Youth Corner

A Collapsed Cart 301

— Translated by Deren

It was almost noon, and the blazing sun shone brightly on a long road. The pedestrians on the road were walking hurriedly and diligently. Suddenly, at the turn of the road, a collapsed cart loaded with bricks appeared. Four people were pulling it in the front, and one person was pushing it in the back. However, because this newly constructed asphalt road was not very sturdy, and the hot sun had softened it a bit, the heavily loaded cart left wheel marks on it from time to time. As the people pulling this cart were about to exert themselves to pull it through the turn, the cart suddenly stopped moving.

The coolies pulling the cart seemed to be very strenuous. Heavy sighs were constantly coming from their open mouths, and beads of sweat were hanging straight down their greasy backs. They continued, still trying hard to push forward, but the wheels seemed to be stuck to the ground, not moving at all. The pedestrians on the road, seeing this cart that could not move forward, gave it a faint sidelong glance and hurried on their way.

Before long, a boy who looked like a shop assistant, about ten years old, walked over from afar, holding a bowl in one hand and a dozen copper coins in the other. He was smugly and playfully knocking the copper coins on the edge of the bowl, while humming lazily from his mouth. The discordant sound mixed with the sound of the copper coins hitting the edge of the bowl.

He slowly walked closer, step by step, and finally stopped in front of the collapsed cart, curiously watching the pulling of these tired coolies.

"Let me help you out. Why can't this cart move at all?" The boy put down the bowl and copper coins, and confidently giggled as he helped them push hard at the back of the cart. But he was a child, and a child's strength, no matter how strong, was ultimately limited and could not compare with that of adults. Although he lowered his head and pushed with all his might, the cart still did not move.

At this time, several children around the age of five or six had already gathered around the collapsed cart.

"Everyone come and help, help! The wheels of this cart seem to be stuck in the asphalt!" This child, who was helping the coolies push, seeing that he was ultimately useless, shouted at others to help.

The weather was so sultry, and the sunlight was so strong. Even half-naked, constantly waving a fan, it still didn't seem cool. Who would be happy to exert themselves again to help these coolies who were unrelated to me, and make themselves sweat unnecessarily?

Children are active, so the children who were gathered around the collapsed cart, seeing that no one came to help, reached out their hands and recklessly went to help. However, the coolies, not appreciating the good intentions, rudely stopped them.

"Thank you, would you please help us push?" These helpless coolies pleaded with the shopkeepers sitting idly in the shops along the road, waving their fans, and with the passers-by wearing short shirts and exposing their sweaty chests.

"Who would be happy to waste their energy pushing for you?" I imagined that the shopkeepers who were watching coldly probably thought this way in their hearts, because you could see this expression from their unmoved, always still faces. And, oh, they were so good at calmly and motionlessly passing the time. If their hand waving the fan hadn't been moving, and their eyes hadn't been rolling, I might have thought they were already dead, already cold, already stiff.

Fortunately, with a chattering sound, several rickshaws carrying people came. When the rickshaw pullers saw that the collapsed cart was blocking their way, they shouted loudly. Later, when they realized that their shouts were useless, they frowned and grumbled as they helped the coolies push hard forward.

The strength of several rickshaw pullers was indeed not small. After they pushed hard a few times, the wheels moved slightly at first, and then, with a "whish" sound, the wheels slid over. The faces of the coolies pulling the collapsed cart immediately showed happy smiles, and they nodded slightly to the rickshaw pullers and went away, raising their voices.

At this time, the children who were gathered around the cart all cheered and ran away, jumping and skipping. The child who had helped the coolies push but couldn't move it also gradually walked away, knocking the edge of the bowl along the way, humming casually. And the shopkeepers next to the counter in the shops along the road were still sitting idly, listlessly, coldly watching a pedestrian's sweaty face... Everything was the same as before, only the turn in the road, covered in sunlight, had one more wheel mark.

Youth Corner Submission Guidelines 302

(1) Articles should be related to discussions of youth issues, news about domestic and foreign schools or students, essays, descriptions of school life, writing as you go, etc. The style is not limited.

(2) Drawings should be limited to those related to youth issues.

(3) Photographs of youth life (such as sports, travel, excursions, plays, lectures, gatherings, exhibitions, various competitions, etc.), school news, and youth movements are acceptable, whether individual or group. Do not write on both sides of the manuscript paper.

(5) Write horizontally and legibly. Omit Western text where possible. If it is necessary to quote the original text, it must be attached below the translation.

Manuscripts will not be returned regardless of whether they are published or not. If you require a return,

Social News

The World's Shoe King—Bata 303

— Written by Fulan

When the son of a village cobbler, whose father carried his tools from door to door soliciting business, who would have thought that the son would today make the industrial circles of Britain and America retreat in deference?

He is Thomas Bata, from Zlin in Moravia, Czechoslovakia, who is the current world shoe king.

Regardless of Bata's thoughts, he is influential enough to affect the markets of Northampton and Leicester in Britain, and the shoe-making towns in America. Because his relationship with the world shoe industry is so great, his plans are eagerly reported by consulates of various countries to twelve countries. Bata's current situation can be briefly summarized as follows:

He has a fortune of ten million pounds.

He owns the world's largest shoe factory, employing 17,000 workers.

He has 2,000 retail stores.

His daily output now exceeds 135,000 pairs.

All of the above success was achieved within ten years.

Today, a large portion of the cheap leather shoes sold to women in Britain are supplied by Bata's factories. He recently signed a contract promising to supply a British company with two million pairs of leather shoes. Recently, he also flew to Berlin to negotiate a batch price, which is to ship five million pairs of leather shoes to the United States.

Whenever he travels in Europe or within his own country, he travels in his own plane, with his own pilot, because "time is money," so he cannot travel by train or steamship.

Speaking of Bata himself, he is a strong, hardworking villager, but he has brains and immeasurable imagination.

Sitting in his office in Zlin, with a rotating globe in front of him, he marks the places he wants to promote Bata shoes. Wherever he marks, his sales organization must reach the goal, otherwise, he must clearly understand the reasons why the goal cannot be reached.

Next to his desk, there is also a signal light, which is used to indicate which of the forty factories he has established is currently in need of his personal presence.

As soon as he sees the signal, he responds to the call. He has such specialized skills that he can restore various machines that have gone wrong, or instruct the foremen on how to handle certain matters.

In Zlin, the efficiency of all work has truly reached the fullest extent, which no British factory has ever dreamed of. As for the Zlin area itself, it is truly a strange town. In the eyes of ordinary people, it is impossible to become an important industrial area, because there is no coal nearby, no waterway transportation, and no railway. Except for a branch line, it is surrounded by chaotic mountains. This is no different from the worst location. No one can imagine that it can become industrialized.

If you travel by train and pass through the countryside, you can see that the workers there still rely on ox power and stick to ancient habits. Then you can see that Zlin is a new-style factory town, with wide roads, beautiful buildings, all using light, air, and electricity.

Forty factories stand in rows behind the grassy and tree-lined roads. Around the factories are small mountains, and workers' apartments and dormitories. Bata's reason for making Zlin the factory center is entirely based on low wages, because the cheap labor in the rural areas of Moravia is truly inexhaustible. He absorbs it, trains it, and utilizes it.

Whether British or American, it is difficult to stand on Bata's massive production and wage system. One cannot imagine that a small village in Moravia has achieved such results.

Every man or woman working in his great factory must work according to a production standard. They must do "so much" every day. If they don't do this, or can't do this, then their wages are regrettable.

Manufacturing leather shoes in the factory is like manufacturing cars, that is, using segmented work—a transfer system. Male and female workers, after completing their work, hand it over to the receiver, which must meet the prescribed standards, otherwise they will be punished.

As for wages, of course, they are very low compared to Britain, but they are much better than the low wages in any town in the Czech Republic. On average, female workers work 45 hours a week and earn fifteen shillings, but they live in dormitories prepared for them by Bata, dance in halls prepared for them by Bata, eat and drink in restaurants prepared for them by Bata, and are entertained in cinemas prepared for them by Bata. At the same time, all food items are purchased from stores opened for them by Bata. Finally, she hands over her savings to Bata and receives interest.

These are the secrets of Bata's success. He arranges the entire town's enjoyment facilities for the citizens, and at the same time they take out money from their earned wages to pay for the cost of his facilities.

According to calculations, every Wednesday, six and a half percent of all wages paid to workers last week have already returned to Bata's pocket through the apartments, restaurants, and shops opened by Bata.

In this town, whatever he builds, he uses his own workers. He controls all procedures of shoe-making, starting from tanning leather all the way to making a complete pair of shoes. He makes his own cardboard boxes, his own wrapping paper, and prints his own catalogs in twelve different languages.

The houses he built around the factory are also generally well-built—requiring only three shillings of rent per week. If a single man or woman can pay one shilling per week, they can live in his dormitory. As for food, the best meal only costs five pence in his restaurant. The cost of entering the cinema is only one penny.

So, a girl, although she only has fifteen shillings of income per week, can live comfortably for seven days for ten shillings, which is much better than working in the mountain village, where she only earns eight shillings per week.

Everything that workers need, from a hat to a motorcycle, is sold in Bata's department store in the town.

The most exquisite building in the town is the workers' club, which has a sports field, a swimming pool, and a very capable hospital. Bata himself lives in a simple villa, which pales in comparison to the workers' club.

The characteristic of the Bata system is that each individual unit must earn its surplus, even in the hospital.

Page 20

They generally have to pay money as well. If there are any losses in any part, he reduces the wages of the workers in that part. In short, they are jointly responsible for joint profits, and joint losses.

Anyone living in Bata's dormitory must live according to rules. They must get up at 5:30 in the morning. After getting up, they must do gymnastics, and at 7:00 they must be at work in the factory.

They must use their spare time in the evening in such and such a way. They must go to bed at 9:00 at night. They are not allowed to smoke or drink alcohol while working.

They must save a portion of their income. When they sign a work contract, they must state all the details of their personal situation, including their debts, assets, and family situation. And Bata is their master, their king, and their world.

The leather shoes made in Bata's factories are the cheapest goods in the world, but although the price is cheap, the quality is very good, so sales are developing day by day. His business is developing every day. At the moment—if the need arises—he can manufacture 200,000 pairs of leather shoes every day, which is more than the daily production of all the large and small factories in Northampton, a famous shoe-producing area in England.

He has a plan to offer to his government. According to this plan, by 1940, Zlin can become a large town with a population of 100,000.

He manufactures leather shoes, but he plans to manufacture other things as well, just as well as he manufactures leather shoes. His unique system has no ethical limits. He is more perfect than Henry Ford and William Morris.

Bata and his uncontrollable efforts of his mind have flown from Central Europe to the world. He specializes in training young men and women. The executives in his factories are rarely older than twenty-five years old.

What level is he going to reach? He accumulated a ten million pound fortune in ten years. He is only fifty-five years old now.

Great Development of Indian Cotton Industry During the National Movement 304

Expanded by 310,000 spindles last year

Bombay News, India's imports of cotton cloth from various countries last year decreased compared to the previous year due to the continued national movement and the adoption of protective policies. At the same time, Indian yarn mills have developed considerably, with the number of yarn spindles expanding by 318,000 last year (China 206,000, Japan 354,000). Especially between July of last year and the end of January of this year, its development status was particularly prominent among yarn mills in the Far East. Compare the list as follows (unit: thousands of spindles)

End of January this year End of June last year Comparison

India 9,125 7,907 218

China 3,905 3,829 76

Japan 7,191 7,072 119

Total 20,221 19,808 413

That is, Indian yarn mills increased by 218,000 spindles in the second half of last year, accounting for more than half of the total of the three countries, China, Japan, and India. This tendency has remained the same recently. It is predicted that the result of the third round table meeting in September of this year will have a more profound impact.

The United States Establishes an Aviation Base in California 305

Facing Singapore and the British Port from Afar - Exerting Pressure on the Pacific Ocean

New York News: The US Navy Department plans to establish a large-scale naval air force base in San Diego, California. The cost is tentatively set at ten million gold dollars. The architectural design drawings have been completed and construction is expected to begin in mid-month. The area will become the most important center of gravity on the Pacific coast. The US Navy calls the area "one eye of the West Coast Defense Fleet," because its terrain protrudes from the West Coast, and the entire coastline stretching thousands of miles is clearly visible, making it extremely valuable for national defense. The ZRS No. 5 and Akion, known as the world's two largest flying ships, are docked in the port. The two ships have a speed of 83 miles per hour and a range of 60 miles, and can be said to be the most powerful force in the Pacific Ocean. Behind the port, a new aviator village has been established, and the families of naval aviators live in the village. The port faces the British naval base in Singapore from afar, exerting pressure on the Pacific Ocean.

Social News

The War Diary of French General Foch 306

— Translated by Gu Angruo

It is surprising to see a plan of operation completed in an instant, and dispersed forces made to act in unison. Those accustomed to the slower pace of trench warfare are inevitably taken aback. Some have doubts about the outcome of this campaign, as many military experts believe that it seemed impossible for Commander Mangin to launch an offensive with five divisions before the twelfth.

As for me, I advocated for a swift counter-attack. Before the enemy had the opportunity to calmly deploy, our troops could catch them off guard. If our army began its counter-attack on the twelfth, the enemy would undoubtedly be prepared by the eleventh.

Commander Mangin's resolute attitude and clear observations ultimately convinced Commander Fayolle. He then issued an order at four o'clock on the afternoon of the tenth, requiring all armies to launch an attack on the eleventh. The order was distributed to all soldiers, with the following concluding remarks: "Our army has been on the defensive for more than two months. Tomorrow's advance will mark the end of the defensive war. This campaign will be the beginning of the German army's exhaustion and our army's regaining the offensive. We vow not to return until we have triumphed. Let all the brave soldiers of the three armies understand this."

Our army indeed began its attack at eleven o'clock on the morning of the following day, the eleventh. In one battle, we successively recaptured the villages of Mailly and Blérancourt, and the entire area along the Longe River was cleared. Over a thousand prisoners were captured, along with a number of artillery pieces.

On the twelfth, I agreed with Commander Pétain at Chantilly that Commander Mangin's advance should be stopped when the opportunity was right, to avoid losses outweighing the gains. It is now necessary to reinforce the remaining troops to prepare for any eventuality. For example, the danger from enemy forces on the Somme River front has not yet been completely eliminated.

The major battle had stopped. On June 14th, there were some local skirmishes. The enemy occupied the left bank of the Meuse River on the eleventh to strengthen their defensive line. The attack west of the Oise River completely ceased.

The enemy's halt west of the Oise River can be regarded as preparation for a large-scale attack east of the river. On the following day, June 12th, two or three German divisions launched a fierce attack north of the Fère-en-Tardenois Forest. Catres and Dommiers successively fell. French troops retreated towards Gouffre and Saint-Pierre-Aigle. However, this was still just a local conflict, and the enemy did not pursue.

On June 13th, the entire French line returned to calm.

The battle that the German army had prepared for this time was actually part of the operational plan that began on May 27th. At the beginning of this battle, the aim was to advance along the entire line between Reims and Montdidier. Due to a lack of heavy artillery and mortars, the German army could not immediately carry out a general offensive along the entire line. All that could be carried out were two battles: one east of the Oise River, the attack on the Chemin des Dames which began on May 27th, and the other, the conflict that was about to occur west of the river. The second battle could not be carried out until the artillery used in the first battle could be moved once it was no longer needed.

However, in the second battle, the German army could no longer gain the advantage they had in the first battle. The hasty operations caused them to neglect the detailed precautions that were taken during the May 27th offensive. Their new plan was thus quickly detected by French reconnaissance aircraft and spies. By May 30th, Commander Pétain had a clear understanding of the enemy's operational plan. The only thing that was not clear was the size of the enemy's forces. It was speculated that the enemy's forces must be very strong, because according to the calculations of the French General Staff, the German High Command had approximately sixty reserve divisions, and no fewer than forty-five divisions were adopting an offensive posture between the Oise River and the Somme River. This was more than the number of troops that launched the attack on the Chemin des Dames on May 27th. In any case, it certainly greatly exceeded the total forces that our army could deploy.

In reality, the German 18th Army (commanded by General Hutier), which was responsible for this battle, had only thirteen divisions deployed on the front line, covering a front of twenty-one miles. On the opposite side, the French army had seven divisions of the 3rd Army (commanded by General Humbert) on the front line, and five divisions in the second line or in reserve. Seven infantry divisions and three cavalry divisions were assembled in the rear between Pont-à-Vendin and Saint-Léonard, which could also be used as reinforcements.

At the start of the battle, these forces were certainly not enough to contain the German attack. However, in the days before the start of the battle, the course of events had not yet been clearly determined, and our army had to prepare for the future.

On June 4th, I warned Haig, "If the enemy continues to advance towards Paris between the Marne River and the Oise River, or if he expands the front, for example, between Thierelles and Montdidier, the Allied forces in France must all join the battle. This battle will determine the fate of the Great War." I asked him to prepare in detail, to transfer all of his remaining troops, the general reserves, and the independent reserve divisions to the front line, and also to consider withdrawing troops from his front. The British General Headquarters therefore had a misunderstanding.

Commander Haig had previously been ordered to transfer three divisions of the 22nd Army Corps to the Somme River front west of Amiens. At the same time, he was still afraid that the enemy would attack between the Lys River and the Somme River (Note: On June 4th, the Bavarian Crown Prince's remaining forces numbered forty-nine divisions, of which twenty-six divisions were fresh troops). He therefore formally protested against the further withdrawal of British troops. He appealed to his home government based on the Pont-à-Vendin Agreement.

On June 7th, a meeting was convened at the office of the Prime Minister in Paris. Those present were Monsieur Clemenceau, Marquis Millerand, Commander Haig, Commander Wilson, Commander Lawrence, Commander Weygand, and myself.

Marquis Millerand said that the British government, like Commander Haig, felt uneasy about the large-scale transfer of British reserve troops, and therefore requested that this meeting be convened. I then calmly replied that I had not yet moved any British reserve troops. I had only asked Commander Haig to consider this matter and to prepare for it in case of an emergency. Moreover, unless it was a truly critical moment, I would never have the intention of exhausting all of his reserve troops.

This incident was easily resolved, but it shows that the Allied Supreme Commander inevitably encounters unexpected difficulties when trying to get the guest armies to understand each other. It is like an orchestra, and it takes considerable time to harmonize and tune the various instruments.

When the generals of the meeting, other issues were raised for discussion. Commander Wilson raised the issue that had occurred during the Battle of Flanders. He asked me what strategy I would adopt for the British army if the German army attacked Paris and simultaneously threatened the British naval base in the North Sea.

I repeated what I had said before. I said that my plan was to firmly maintain the connection between the French and British armies, to defend Paris and protect the coast, because they were mutually dependent on the success or failure of the Great War.

Disregarding the panic of the people and the governments of various countries, the enemy would soon achieve one of their primary objectives, either Paris or the Channel ports. This was indeed the case. At that time, the Allied forces were only engaged in defense, and they were inevitably exhausted. From the commander to the soldiers, no one did not feel that the crisis was imminent, and each had a sense of impending doom. (To be continued)
